● Skill(grill-me)
  ⎿  Successfully loaded skill

● Let me start grilling. I'll go one question at a time, deepest-first.

  ---
  Question 1: Your PR risk score contradicts your core thesis.

  You opened with "impact is NOT measured by LOC, commits, or PR counts" — yet
  your PR risk score = changes + adds + deletes. That's literally lines of code
  with a relabel. And you want to take the median of it as a quality signal
  where "low is better."

  This creates a perverse incentive: an engineer who deletes 5,000 lines of dead
   code in one PR (huge impact) gets penalized as "high risk." An engineer who
  ships 20 trivial one-line PRs looks "low risk" and high throughput.

  My recommendation: Risk shouldn't be size — it should be blast radius. Better
  signals:
  - Files touched across module boundaries (touching posthog/, frontend/, and
  plugin-server/ in one PR = high risk)
  - Touches critical paths (migrations, auth, billing, ingestion pipeline —
  these have known historical incident density)
  - Test-to-code ratio in the diff (large code change with no test changes =
  risk)
  - Review latency under load (merged fast despite being large = risk)

  And impact should be measured separately from risk. A high-impact engineer
  might ship risky things — that's fine if quality holds. The dashboard should
  show impact-adjusted-for-quality, not penalize size directly.

  Do you want to:
  1. Keep risk = LOC (and accept the contradiction)
  2. Redefine risk as blast radius (files × criticality × test coverage),
  decoupled from size
  3. Drop "risk" as a top-level metric entirely and fold it into Quality

  Which direction — and why?

✻ Brewed for 21s

❯ yeah agree about the refactoring because like if someone is deleting like     
  docs/readme then that would be incorrect risk assesment. here one thing we    
  have make use how do we defince criticality                                   

● Good — you've landed on blast radius. Now the hard part: criticality is the 
  load-bearing piece of that whole definition, and it's where most dashboards
  quietly fail because they hand-wave it.

  ---
  Question 2: How do you define criticality without hand-coding a list of 
  "important folders"?

  Three approaches, each with real tradeoffs:

  Option A — Hand-curated path weights (config file).
  You (or a PostHog tech lead) writes: posthog/migrations/* = 1.0, posthog/api/*
   = 0.8, frontend/src/scenes/billing/* = 0.9, docs/* = 0.0. Simple,
  transparent, defensible to leadership.
  Cost: requires domain knowledge of PostHog's repo. Stale the moment they
  reorg.
  Honesty: this is what real engineering metrics teams actually do.

  Option B — Derived from incident/revert history.
  Files that appear in reverted PRs or in PRs tagged bug/hotfix over the last 12
   months get weighted higher. "Criticality = empirical historical pain."
  Cost: circular — your Quality pillar already uses reverts, so you're
  double-counting. Also noisy on small repos/files.

  Option C — Derived from churn × author breadth.
  Files modified frequently by many different authors = core/critical. Files
  touched rarely or by one person = peripheral. PageRank-style.
  Cost: conflates "active development area" with "critical." A new feature
  folder under heavy dev would look critical when it's actually green-field.

  My recommendation: A + a small dose of C as a sanity check.
  Hand-curate ~5–8 path globs with weights for PostHog (migrations, ingestion,
  billing, auth, query engine). Everything else defaults to 1.0. Then validate
  by checking: do the top-criticality files also show up in C's churn-breadth
  ranking? If not, your hand-curation is wrong.

  Don't use B — it leaks Quality signal into Risk.

  For PostHog specifically, I'd guess the high-criticality globs are:
  - posthog/migrations/**
  - plugin-server/** (event ingestion — outages here are user-visible)
  - posthog/api/** (public API surface)
  - posthog/models/** (schema-adjacent)
  - ee/billing/** (revenue)

  Which option — A, B, C, or a blend? And do you want me to actually clone 
  PostHog and validate the criticality globs against real revert history before 
  you commit to weights?

● Agreed — config-driven with sensible defaults, user-overridable. That's the
  right call for a generic tool. Locking that in.

  ---
  Question 3: What does this dashboard actually output — and is "rank engineers 
  by composite score" the right shape at all?

  You've designed a weighted composite: Delivery 35 + Collaboration 35 + Quality
   30 = single score per engineer. Busy leaders see a ranked list. This is the
  most common shape — and it's also where most impact dashboards fail in ways
  that hurt the org:

  The known failure modes:
  1. Goodhart's law: the moment engineers see the ranking, they optimize the
  metric, not the work. Self-merge rate drops because people ping a friend for a
   rubber-stamp. PR risk drops because people split work into smaller PRs. The
  signal degrades within a quarter.
  2. Composite scores hide the thing leaders need to see. "Alice 7.2, Bob 6.8"
  tells the leader nothing actionable. Why is Bob lower? Was it one bad month?
  Is he doing review-heavy work (low Delivery, high Collaboration) that the
  weighting punishes?
  3. Role bias. A senior engineer reviewing 40 PRs/week and mentoring scores low
   on Delivery. A junior shipping CRUD endpoints scores high. The composite says
   the wrong thing.
  4. Your stated audience contradicts the shape. "Busy leaders who don't review
  every PR" don't need a leaderboard — they need anomaly detection: "who's
  stuck, who's carrying invisible load, where is the team's review bottleneck."

  Three alternative shapes:

  Shape A — Ranked composite score (your current plan). Familiar, easy to read,
  politically toxic, gameable.

  Shape B — Per-engineer profile cards, no ranking. Each engineer gets a radar
  chart across the three pillars + a short narrative ("Carries heavy review
  load; ships rarely but high-criticality"). Leaders scan for patterns not
  positions. No single number.

  Shape C — Team-level health + outlier callouts. Dashboard shows team
  aggregates (review turnaround, revert rate, cycle time) and surfaces
  individuals only when they're outliers worth a 1:1 conversation ("Carol's
  review turnaround is 4× team median — is she overloaded?"). Most honest for
  the stated audience.

  My recommendation: B with a dash of C. Per-engineer profiles (no overall
  rank), plus a "things worth a conversation" panel that flags outliers in
  either direction (heroes carrying invisible load, and people who might need
  support). No single composite number. If leadership demands a ranking, give
  them the three pillar scores side-by-side and let them weight in their head —
  that forces them to think instead of trust a number.

  The 35/35/30 weighting is also suspicious — it's precise enough to look
  researched but arbitrary enough that nobody can defend the difference between
  35 and 30. If you keep weights, make them user-configurable with the same
  "default + override" pattern as criticality.

  Which shape — A, B, or C? And are you willing to drop the single composite 
  score, or does leadership specifically want one number per person?

  Question 4: Half your KPIs can't be measured from GitHub. Which do we drop, 
  which do we proxy, which do we punt?

  KPI: Delivery per headcount
  Verdict: Keep
  Why: Team-level, derivable (merged PRs / active authors over window). Useful.
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: Defect rate
  Verdict: Keep, narrow
  Why: You already have it: reverts + bug-labeled follow-ups. Don't redefine it.
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: AI-assisted code
  Verdict: Drop
  Why: Not detectable from GitHub. Co-authored-by trailers are unreliable,
    optional, and gameable. Any number you show here is fiction.
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: Collaboration
  Verdict: Keep
  Why: Already a pillar.
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: Innovation rate
  Verdict: Drop or redefine
  Why: "Innovation" has no GitHub signal. The closest proxy is "PRs introducing
    new top-level modules/features" — but that's just feature-vs-bug mix, which
    you already have. Don't add a metric you can't define.
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: Roadmap alignment
  Verdict: Drop
  Why: Requires linking PRs to a roadmap (Linear/Jira/OKRs). Generic GitHub tool

  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: Cycle time
  Verdict: Keep
  Why: Already in Delivery pillar. PR open → merge.
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: Capitalizable
  Verdict: Drop
  Why: This is a finance/accounting concept (CapEx vs OpEx for software dev).
    Requires HR + finance data. Has no place in an engineering impact dashboard.
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: Talent density
  Verdict: Drop
  Why: Not a measurable metric — it's a vibe. Even if you defined it (% senior
    engineers? review breadth?), it's not what GitHub data tells you.
  ────────────────────────────────────────
  KPI: Knowledge gaps
  Verdict: Keep, redefine
  Why: Real signal: bus factor per critical path. "Only 1 person has touched
    plugin-server/ingestion/ in 6 months." This is genuinely useful for leaders
    and derivable.

  The pattern: your original 3-pillar design (Delivery/Collaboration/Quality)
  was tight and defensible. This 10-KPI list adds vague management vocabulary
  that sounds impressive in a slide but will either be uncomputable or will be
  computed on bad proxies that mislead leaders.

  My recommendation:
  - Keep & merge into pillars: Delivery-per-headcount → Delivery. Defect rate →
  Quality. Cycle time → Delivery. Collaboration → existing pillar.
  - Add as new signal: Knowledge gaps / bus factor — this is genuinely valuable
  and not in your original design.
  - Drop entirely: AI-assisted, Innovation rate, Roadmap alignment,
  Capitalizable, Talent density. Each of these would force you to fake a number.

  The discipline here: a metric you can't honestly compute is worse than no 
  metric, because leaders make decisions on it.

  Do you accept this triage? Specifically — are you willing to drop AI-assisted,
   Innovation, Capitalizable, Talent density, and Roadmap alignment, or is one 
  of them a hard requirement from a stakeholder?

✻ Baked for 24s

❯ my plan is to add ai summary for each engineer and I agree we cannot check    
  whole code for ai assisted.                                                   

● Good — AI summary per engineer. That's a useful narrative layer for busy
  leaders who don't want to read radar charts. But it's the easiest place in
  this whole dashboard to generate confident-sounding nonsense, so let's nail it
   down.

  ---
  Question 5: What goes into the AI summary prompt — and how do you stop it from
   hallucinating?

  Three real options:

  Option A — Feed raw PR titles + descriptions + diffs.
  Most "informed" output. Catastrophic on cost (PostHog has 10k+ PRs), latency
  (minutes per engineer), and context window. Also: the LLM will pattern-match
  prose quality ("Alice writes detailed PR descriptions") into impact judgments,
   which is exactly the bias you're trying to avoid.

  Option B — Feed only the computed metrics (the pillar scores + sub-metrics).
  Cheap, fast, deterministic-ish. But the summary becomes a thesaurus exercise:
  "Alice has high Delivery (8.2) and low Quality (5.1)" → "Alice ships
  consistently but with some quality concerns." Adds zero information beyond the
   numbers themselves. Leaders will (rightly) ignore it.

  Option C — Feed metrics + a curated sample of evidence: top 5 PR titles by 
  criticality, list of files most-touched, bus-factor flags, recent reverts they
   authored.
  Grounded in real artifacts the LLM can cite. Output looks like: "Carries the 
  ingestion pipeline (sole author of 12 PRs in plugin-server/ingestion/ over the
   last quarter — bus factor risk). Two reverts in March traced to a migration. 
  Reviews heavily across frontend, suggesting cross-team mentoring." That's the
  kind of summary a leader actually uses.

  My recommendation: C, with hard guardrails.

  Specifically:
  1. Prompt only includes data you can cite. PR titles + numbers + file paths.
  No diffs.
  2. Require the LLM to cite PR numbers / file paths inline. If it can't cite,
  it can't claim. This is the single biggest hallucination defense.
  3. Structured output, not free prose. Three buckets: Strengths (with 
  evidence), Concerns (with evidence), Watch-items. Forces the model to attach
  claims to artifacts.
  4. Cache aggressively. Regenerate weekly, not on dashboard load. Per-engineer
  summaries on every page load = burning money for stale data.
  5. Show the leader the evidence the summary was generated from, collapsed by
  default. So they can sanity-check when something feels off.

  The trap to avoid: don't let the LLM see the composite score and then write a
  summary justifying it. That's reverse-reasoning — the score becomes the
  conclusion the summary defends, and you've laundered a number into a
  narrative. Generate the summary from raw evidence, then show the score next to
   it.

  Which option — A, B, or C? And are you OK with weekly batch regeneration vs 
  on-demand per dashboard view (cost matters a lot here — Opus per-engineer 
  per-load on a 50-engineer team gets expensive fast)?

  ---
  Question 6: "Top contributor" by what — and doesn't this resurrect the 
  leaderboard you wanted GitHub-style?

  You said earlier you wanted a GitHub-contributor-card composite, and I
  conceded. Now "Top Yearly/Quarterly/Monthly Contributor" turns that composite
  into a Hall of Fame ranking — which is the most gameable, most politically
  charged version of the leaderboard pattern. One name at the top, frozen in
  time, in three time windows.

  Real risks:
  1. Recency bias. Someone who shipped a huge migration in week 1 of the quarter
   dominates the quarterly view; someone doing slow, deep work on a refactor
  lands nowhere.
  2. Reviewers always lose. A senior engineer reviewing 80 PRs/quarter and
  unblocking the team will rank lower than someone shipping medium-criticality
  features. So "Top Contributor" becomes "Top Shipper" — the exact thing you
  said you didn't want.
  3. Single winner is the worst shape. Top 5 with their distinct strengths is
  more honest.

  My recommendation:
  - Replace "Top Contributor (singular)" with "Standouts" (3–5 people, each with
   a one-line reason): "Carried ingestion (bus factor)", "Highest review
  throughput", "Most cross-team breadth", "Lowest revert rate among
  high-shippers". Different axes, not one composite.
  - Keep monthly/quarterly/yearly time windows — that's fine.
  - Make the reason the headline, not the name. "Carried ingestion: Carol" reads
   very differently from "🏆 Top Contributor: Carol."

  This way you keep the recognition value (leaders love seeing standouts)
  without recreating the gameable single-number ranking.

  ---
  Now — I still need you to close Question 5. AI summary inputs:
  - A (raw PRs + diffs — expensive, hallucination-prone),
  - B (just metrics — useless thesaurus),
  - C (metrics + cited evidence: top PR titles, file paths, reverts, bus-factor
  flags — grounded, structured, cached weekly)?

  And: on-demand regeneration vs weekly batch? Cost matters — 50 engineers ×
  Opus per dashboard view adds up fast.

  Please answer both: (a) Standouts vs single Top Contributor, and (b) A/B/C + 
  caching strategy.

● Locked: standouts + option C + weekly cache with manual refresh. Good.

  ---
  Question 7: Who counts as an "engineer" on this dashboard?

  This is the unsexy plumbing question that breaks dashboards on day one.
  PostHog's repo (and most real repos) contains:

  - Bots: dependabot, renovate, github-actions, posthog-bot. They will appear at
   the top of every "PRs merged" metric if you don't filter them.
  - External / drive-by contributors: someone who fixed one typo six months ago.
   Do they appear in the dashboard? They'll have terrible Collaboration scores
  (no reviews given) for unfair reasons.
  - Former employees: someone who left in March still has authored PRs from
  January. Do they show up in the quarterly standouts? Awkward if HR-relevant.
  - Multi-account humans: the same person committing as alice@company.com and
  alice@personal.com. GitHub's API gives you both as separate contributors.
  - Joiners / leavers within the window: someone who started in week 8 of a
  12-week quarter has 4 weeks of data. Comparing them on absolute throughput is
  meaningless. Comparing on rates (PRs/week) over-weights their small sample.
  - Role mismatches: designers who occasionally commit, managers who sometimes
  ship, SREs whose work is mostly config not features.

  Real failure modes if you don't resolve this:
  - Dependabot wins "Top Quarterly Contributor."
  - A new hire's 3-week sample produces a wild outlier score that misleads their
   manager.
  - A senior engineer on parental leave appears as "low Delivery, high Quality"
  — technically true, completely useless signal.

  My recommendation — the minimum viable cohort definition:

  1. Bot exclusion list (config-driven, with defaults). Default-exclude any
  login matching *[bot], dependabot*, renovate*, github-actions*, plus a config
  override list. Same pattern as criticality.
  2. Active-engineer threshold. Only include authors with ≥ N merged PRs and ≥ M
   weeks of activity in the window. Default N=3, M=4. Below that, they're
  "occasional contributors" — show them in a separate section, not in
  standouts/rankings.
  3. Tenure-aware normalization. All rate metrics (PRs/week, reviews/week)
  computed against active weeks in window, not calendar weeks. Someone with 4
  weeks of activity is compared on per-week rates, not totals.
  4. Identity dedup: punt to v2. GitHub login is the unit. If a human has two
  logins, they'll show as two people. Don't try to be clever — email-based
  merging gets people's identities wrong, which is worse than showing two cards.
  5. Don't try to detect leavers. If someone has zero activity in the last 4
  weeks of the window, surface that as a flag ("inactive in recent period")
  rather than excluding — the leader knows their team better than you do.

  One sharper question inside this: for review-given metrics (Collaboration
  pillar), do you include reviews of bot PRs and external-contributor PRs? My
  take: no for bots, yes for external. Reviewing a dependabot bump is
  rubber-stamp work; reviewing an external contributor's PR is real mentorship.
  But this needs to be a config knob.

  Do you accept this cohort definition? Specifically:
  - (a) bot exclusion via config + defaults — yes/no?
  - (b) min-activity threshold for inclusion in rankings (default 3 PRs / 4
  weeks) — yes/no, or different threshold?
  - (c) rate-based normalization on active weeks — yes/no?
  - (d) identity dedup — punt to v2, yes/no?

✻ Cooked for 39s

❯ Accept (a) yes, (b) yes with configurable N/M (maybe M=3 for monthly), (c)    
  yes, (d) yes punt v2, plus configurable review-eligibility rules for bot vs   
  external PRs.                                                                 

● Locked. M=3 for monthly is the right tweak — small windows need lower
  thresholds.

  ---
  Question 8: Your Quality pillar relies on linking bug fixes back to the PR 
  that caused them. How?

  You wrote: "PRs reverted within 2 weeks, PRs followed by bug fixes."

  The first half is fine — git revert commits are detectable, and you can walk
  back to the original PR. ~2% of PRs in most repos. Useful but sparse signal.

  The second half — "PRs followed by bug fixes" — is the load-bearing bit, and
  it's also the part nobody can compute reliably. To attribute a bug to its
  causing PR you need one of:

  Option A — Linked issues. Bug-fix PR closes issue #123, which references PR
  #100 as the cause. Requires disciplined linking that PostHog's repo might have
   but most repos don't. Even at PostHog, coverage is partial. Recall: low. 
  Precision: high.

  Option B — git blame on the bug-fix diff. When a fix lands, blame the lines it
   modified. The PR that last touched those lines = "likely cause." Used by
  SonarSource, Code Climate, etc.
  Catches: clear regressions.
  Misses: bugs where the fix lives in different code than the cause (most
  non-trivial bugs). Also blames refactors that touched lines without changing
  logic. Recall: medium. Precision: medium-low.

  Option C — Label heuristics. Count PRs labeled bug/hotfix/fix per author, but
  DON'T attribute back to a causing PR. Just a per-engineer "bug-fix ratio."
  Honest: doesn't pretend to know causation. Useless for the metric you want
  (which is "Bob's PRs cause downstream bugs"). Recall: N/A. Precision: N/A — 
  different metric entirely.

  Option D — Don't do it. Acknowledge that "PRs followed by bug fixes" is
  uncomputable from GitHub data with acceptable accuracy, and remove it from the
   pillar.

  The brutal truth: Option B is what most "DevEx analytics" vendors quietly use,
   and it produces a noisy enough signal that engineers correctly distrust it.
  You blame Alice for a bug because she touched the file three months ago doing
  an unrelated rename. She gets a Quality ding she can't refute. That's how
  dashboards get killed politically.

  My recommendation: B + Option D blend, with explicit honesty.

  1. Compute revert-within-2-weeks as the primary defect signal. It's noisy (low
   recall) but high-precision and uncontroversial — a revert is unambiguous.
  2. Compute git blame-based attribution but only show it as a team-level 
  signal, never per-engineer. Team's regression rate, hot files with frequent
  post-merge fixes — these are useful. Per-engineer attribution via blame is too
   noisy to be fair.
  3. Per-engineer bug-fix ratio (Option C) as a neutral metric in the profile —
  not weighted into Quality score, just shown alongside. Doing lots of bug-fix
  work is valuable; we shouldn't punish or reward it, just surface it.
  4. Be loud about what you're not measuring. A "Quality" pillar that pretends
  to know causation is worse than one that says "we measure reverts and review
  hygiene; deeper defect attribution requires issue-tracker integration not
  available in this version."

  One more thing: if you keep "revert within 2 weeks" — be careful about what 
  counts as a revert. A revert that's immediately followed by a fix-forward
  (re-landing the change correctly) shouldn't count as harshly as a permanent
  revert. Default: penalize all reverts equally, but config-knob to weight
  permanent reverts higher.

  Do you accept:
  - (a) Reverts as primary defect signal — keep
  - (b) Blame-based attribution at team-level only, never per-engineer — accept
  this constraint?
  - (c) Bug-fix ratio shown but unweighted — accept?
  - (d) Drop "PRs followed by bug fixes" as a per-engineer scored metric?

  Or do you want to keep per-engineer blame attribution and accept the political
   cost?

✻ Brewed for 33s

❯ Keep (a), accept (b) and (c), do (d) for scored causation, and avoid          
  per-engineer blame scoring because we do not want to keep blame scoring       

● Locked. No per-engineer blame, team-level only. Clean call.

  ---
  Question 9: "Review depth" is half your Collaboration pillar and you haven't 
  defined it. This is the single most-faked metric in dev analytics.

  You wrote: "review depth + turnaround + breadth + changes-requested rigor"

  Turnaround (time-to-first-review) and breadth (distinct authors reviewed) are
  easy and honest. The other two — depth and changes-requested rigor — collapse
  the moment engineers know they're measured.

  The naive definitions and how they fail:

  - "Comment count per review" → engineers post emoji reactions and "looks
  good!" replies to inflate count. Within a quarter, the metric is noise.
  - "Total comment LOC" → triggers verbose nitpicking. Productivity drops,
  "depth" goes up. Anti-signal.
  - "Time spent reviewing" (from GitHub timestamps) → unreliable. Someone opens
  a PR tab, walks to lunch, comes back. Looks like a 90-minute deep review.
  - "Changes requested rate" → engineers gatekeep harder to bump rigor scores.
  Or the opposite: a senior reviewing junior code legitimately requests changes
  80% of the time and looks "rigorous"; a senior reviewing a peer requests
  changes 10% and looks like a rubber stamp. Same person, different ratios,
  neither tells you about quality.

  The honest options:

  Option A — Composite of weak proxies, accept the noise.
  depth = log(comment_count) × log(comment_chars) × distinct_files_commented_on.
   Imperfect but harder to game than any single one. Most vendors do this. The
  number is roughly right at the team aggregate, roughly meaningless per-PR.

  Option B — Define depth as reviews that produced change.
  A review is "deep" if the PR was modified after the review (commits added
  between review and merge). Reviews that produced no diff change = rubber stamp
   (whether or not they had comments). This is structural, hard to game, and
  aligns with what "depth" actually means.
  Catch: a perfect PR that needed no changes will be scored as a shallow review.
   So pair it with comment-count as a secondary signal — high comments + no
  changes can mean "thorough review of clean PR."

  Option C — Drop "depth" entirely. Keep turnaround, breadth, and 
  changes-requested ratio (without calling it rigor).
  Acknowledge depth isn't measurable from API metadata. Replace it with review 
  load (PRs reviewed per active week) — a load metric is a real thing that
  affects team health and isn't gameable in the same way. Heavy reviewers are
  visible; that's the signal leaders care about.

  My recommendation: B + C blend.

  - Primary depth proxy: "reviews that triggered code change" — % of an
  engineer's reviews where the PR was modified after their review comments.
  Structural, hard to fake.
  - Secondary signal: comment volume, shown but not heavily weighted — to handle
   the "clean PR, no changes needed" case.
  - Replace "changes-requested rigor" with simple changes-requested rate,
  displayed as a neutral statistic in the profile, not scored. High and low are
  both legitimate depending on who you review for.
  - Add review load (PRs reviewed / active week) as a first-class Collaboration
  metric. This is the invisible work leaders most need to see.

  The principle: for any metric you can't compute honestly, either redefine it
  structurally (B) or replace it with an adjacent metric you can compute (C).
  Don't ship a faked depth number.

  Do you accept:
  - (a) Depth = "reviews that triggered code change" + comment volume as
  secondary
  - (b) Changes-requested rate shown but unscored
  - (c) Add review load (PRs/active-week) as a scored Collaboration metric
  - (d) Drop the "rigor" framing — it's a vibe word, not a metric
✻ Crunched for 31s

❯ as the right direction for a dashboard                                        

● Locked: B+C blend on review depth, changes-requested unscored, review-load
  first-class, "rigor" gone.

  ---
  Question 10: Your "feature vs bug PR mix" lens — how do you classify without 
  reliable labels?

  You said this would help leaders decide "who to pick for new feature vs bug
  fix" based on engineer decision-making patterns. Useful framing — but
  classification is the hard part, and most repos (PostHog included) have
  inconsistent labeling.

  The reality of label data:
  - A subset of PRs have bug/feature/enhancement labels. Often <50%.
  - Labels are author-applied and inconsistent. One team uses bug, another uses
  fix, another uses nothing.
  - Conventional Commits prefixes (feat:, fix:, chore:) are more reliable than
  labels in repos that adopt them — title-based.
  - Linked issues sometimes carry the type, but only if PRs link issues (often
  they don't).

  Options:

  A — Trust labels only. Classify only PRs with explicit labels. Drop the rest
  from the lens.
  Honest, sparse. Maybe 40% coverage. The lens is real but partial.

  B — Title heuristics + labels. Combine: Conventional Commits prefix → label →
  keyword match in title (fix, bug, feat, add, revert). Fall back to "unknown."
  Better coverage (~70%), some misclassification. fix typo in docs is
  technically a fix but trivial.

  C — LLM classification on title + body. Send PR title and first 500 chars of
  body to a cheap model, classify into {feature, bugfix, refactor, chore, docs,
  infra}. Cache forever per PR.
  High coverage, high accuracy, real cost. But cost is bounded — you classify
  each PR exactly once.

  D — Three-bucket simplification. Don't try to distinguish refactor / chore /
  infra. Just: {adds capability, fixes defect, everything else}. Easier to
  classify and matches what the leader actually wants to know.

  My recommendation: D as the bucket scheme + B as the primary classifier + C as
   a fallback for the "unknown" bucket.

  Specifically:
  1. Three buckets only: Capability (adds/changes user-facing or API behavior),
  Fix (corrects broken behavior), Maintenance (everything else: refactor, chore,
   infra, docs, test). Leaders care about the first two; the third is context.
  2. Classifier waterfall: Conventional Commits prefix → label match → title
  keyword regex → "unknown."
  3. Optional LLM pass on unknowns, cached per PR forever. Config-gated since it
   costs money.
  4. Show the mix as descriptive, not scored. "Alice: 60% Capability / 25% Fix /
   15% Maintenance." Don't reward Capability over Fix — that re-creates the bias
   against people who keep the lights on. The lens is for staffing decisions,
  not performance scoring.
  5. Surface classification confidence. If only 40% of an engineer's PRs were
  classifiable, say so. Don't paint a confident pie chart on weak data.

  One sharper warning: be very careful framing this as "decision-making
  patterns." If the dashboard says "Bob does mostly bug fixes," a leader might
  infer Bob prefers bug fixes — when actually the team keeps assigning him bugs
  because he's good at them. The metric reflects assignment, not preference.
  Label the lens carefully — "Work mix" not "Decision-making pattern."

  Do you accept:
  - (a) Three buckets: Capability / Fix / Maintenance
  - (b) Waterfall classifier (CC prefix → label → keyword → unknown), with
  optional LLM pass on unknowns
  - (c) Mix shown as descriptive, never scored or weighted into composite
  - (d) "Work mix" framing, not "decision-making patterns"

✻ Worked for 28s

❯ also for previous question i think  (a)–(d) as the right direction for a      
  dashboard and for current I thin B + D + optional C plan would be good        

● Locked Q9 (a-d). Q10: B classifier + D buckets + optional C fallback — and
  I'll lock the descriptive-not-scored framing and "Work mix" label as the
  defaults too unless you push back.

  ---
  Question 11: How do you define cycle time? There are three common definitions 
  and they produce wildly different numbers.

  You listed cycle time as a Delivery sub-metric without specifying. The choice
  is load-bearing because cycle time gets cited in board decks — "our cycle time
   is 2.3 days" — and the underlying definition usually gets lost.

  The three real definitions:

  A — PR open → merge. What every off-the-shelf tool reports. Easy. Honest about
   itself. Misses everything before the PR existed — the design work, the local
  iteration, the draft phase. Engineers who think hard before opening look slow
  on this metric.

  B — First commit on branch → merge. Captures local development time. Closer to
   "real" cycle time. Penalizes long-lived branches even when work was paused
  for legit reasons (waiting on dependency, switched contexts). Also harder to
  compute — needs branch history walking.

  C — Issue opened → merge (DORA-style "lead time for changes"). The most
  "honest" — captures the full delay from "we decided to do this" to "it's
  shipped." Requires issue linkage (often missing) and conflates queueing time
  (sat in backlog 3 months) with actual work time. The 3-month sat-in-backlog
  will dominate any signal.

  The trap: if you pick A, you under-measure work that was carefully designed.
  Pick B, you punish multi-day work paused for legit reasons. Pick C, your
  numbers are dominated by backlog age and tell you nothing about engineering
  velocity.

  My recommendation: A as primary, B as secondary, C dropped.

  1. PR open → merge as the headline cycle-time metric. Documented honestly:
  "time from PR opened to merged." Don't claim it's "lead time."
  2. Within that, decompose into stages:
    - PR opened → first review (review wait)
    - First review → last commit (iteration)
    - Last commit → merge (merge wait)
  This is what leaders actually need. A 5-day cycle time made of 4 days of
  "waiting for review" is a team problem; 4 days of "iteration after review" is
  a different problem entirely.
  3. Exclude draft time. Time spent in draft state doesn't count — that's
  intentional WIP. Start the clock at "ready for review."
  4. Use median, not mean. One stalled PR (open 3 months, abandoned, then
  merged) destroys means. Median is honest.
  5. Drop issue-to-merge. It conflates product/PM decision lag with engineering
  lag. Out of scope for an engineering impact dashboard.
  6. Skip B entirely. Branch-history walking adds complexity for a metric most
  leaders won't use.

  One concrete edge case: what about PRs that sit open for 60+ days? They're
  outliers that distort everything. My recommendation: cap window-included PRs 
  at the analysis window length. A PR open longer than the window doesn't get a
  cycle time recorded — it gets flagged as "stuck." Stuck PRs become their own
  signal.

  Do you accept:
  - (a) PR open → merge, decomposed into review-wait / iteration / merge-wait
  stages
  - (b) Exclude draft time
  - (c) Median, not mean
  - (d) Drop issue-to-merge (DORA-style) — out of scope
  - (e) PRs open longer than analysis window flagged as "stuck," not given a
  cycle time

✻ Baked for 36s

❯ A + staged decomposition + median + draft exclusion + no issue-to-merge seems 
   good for B i think we would have be bear branch history cause                

● Reading "we'd have to bear the branch-history cost" — agreed, B's complexity
  isn't worth it. Skipping B. Locked: A + staged decomposition + median + draft
  exclusion + no issue-to-merge + stuck-PR flagging.

  ---
  Question 12: "CI cleanliness" — what does this actually measure, and do you 
  even have the data?

  You listed CI cleanliness in the Quality pillar. Two problems before we get to
   definition:

  Data availability. GitHub's REST/GraphQL APIs do expose check runs and
  workflow runs per commit/PR — so the data is technically there. But:
  - It's a separate API surface from PRs/reviews. More auth scopes, rate limits,
   paginated heavily.
  - For a generic tool (your stated goal), you can't assume the repo even uses
  GitHub Actions. Some repos use Buildkite, CircleCI, Jenkins via webhooks.
  Those check runs do show up via the Checks API, but with less metadata.
  - Historical CI data has retention limits (90 days for Actions logs by
  default; check run metadata persists longer but isn't infinite).

  Definitional ambiguity. "CI cleanliness" can mean wildly different things:

  A — Failed checks per PR. How many CI runs failed before the PR finally passed
   and merged. Higher = author pushed broken code repeatedly.
  Catch: punishes engineers working on flaky test suites, or testing locally is
  hard, or CI catches integration issues that can't be caught locally. Also:
  someone who pushes work-in-progress commits to a draft PR will look terrible
  vs someone who only pushes when local tests pass.

  B — Merged-with-failing-checks rate. How often this engineer merges PRs while
  required checks were failing or skipped. Direct quality signal.
  Catch: sparse data — most repos require checks to pass. Will show ~0 for
  everyone except in repos with bypass culture, where it's a team problem more
  than individual.

  C — First-attempt pass rate. % of PRs where the first CI run on the final
  commit passed cleanly.
  Catch: same flaky-test punishment as A, but cleaner to compute.

  D — Drop CI cleanliness from per-engineer scoring entirely. Treat CI health as
   a team/repo signal, not an individual one. Show flaky-test rate, average
  build time, % of PRs with green CI on first try — at the team level.

  My recommendation: D, with a small carve-out.

  1. No per-engineer CI score. CI failure correlates much more strongly with
  test suite flakiness and infra than with engineer behavior. Scoring engineers
  on it punishes the wrong people — usually the ones working in the most complex
   / least-tested areas.
  2. Surface CI signals at the team level only:
    - Test suite flakiness rate (% of CI runs that pass on retry without code
  changes)
    - Median time-to-green on PRs
    - % of PRs merged with green CI on first try (team-wide, not per-engineer)
  3. One per-engineer carve-out: "merged with failing required checks" count,
  surfaced as a flag, not a score. If someone merges 5 PRs with bypassed checks,
   that's worth a conversation. Show the flag, don't fold it into Quality score.
  4. Make CI integration optional. Generic tool — many repos won't have rich
  check data. Degrade gracefully: if no check data, hide the CI panel, don't
  fake a metric.
  5. Don't promise this in v1 if it's not core. CI data fetching is a meaningful
   engineering investment (separate API surface, pagination, auth). If your v1
  timeline is tight, push CI cleanliness to v2 and note the gap honestly in the
  Quality pillar.

  The deeper principle: CI failures are system signals, not individual signals.
  A dashboard that scores engineers on CI is mistaking environmental noise for
  personal quality.

  Do you accept:
  - (a) No per-engineer CI scoring — team-level only
  - (b) Optional integration; degrade gracefully when CI data isn't available
  - (c) "Merged with failing checks" as a flag (not score) for individual
  conversations
  - (d) Push CI cleanliness to v2 if v1 timeline is tight — yes/no?

✻ Brewed for 37s

❯ accept all except cavet c only flag when you can reliably detect required     
  checks / branch protection context; otherwise you’ll false-positive on        
  optional checks or transient reds. Treat it as “worth reviewing with Eng      
  leadership”, not a ranking input.                                             

● Excellent caveat — accepted. Flag only when required-check / branch-protection
   context is reliably detectable; otherwise suppress. Frame as "worth reviewing
   with Eng leadership," not score input. Locked.

  ---
  Question 13: You accepted "bus factor / knowledge gaps" as a new metric back 
  in Q4 — but we never defined it. This is the metric leaders most want and most
   often see done badly.

  The intuition is right: "only one person has touched the ingestion pipeline in
   6 months" is gold for a leader. But the definition is fragile.

  The naive definitions and how they fail:

  - "Files only one person has committed to" → 80% of files in any repo. Most
  files are touched once and never again. The metric is meaningless because it's
   mostly true.
  - "Authors per file in last N days" → most files are inactive in any window.
  Filtering to active files helps, but cliff-edges: a file touched 91 days ago
  vs 89 ago shouldn't classify differently.
  - "Author concentration weighted by file criticality" → better, but inherits
  all the criticality-config caveats from Q2.

  The structural question hidden here: bus factor of what?

  - Per file is too granular and noisy.
  - Per directory / module is the right unit — leaders think in terms of "the
  billing system," "the ingestion pipeline."
  - But what defines a module? Top-level dirs? Convention varies wildly. PostHog
   has posthog/, frontend/, plugin-server/, ee/, etc. — but inside posthog/ are
  dozens of sub-areas.

  The options:

  A — Top-level + one-level-deep directories as the module unit. Compute author
  concentration for each. Default cutoff: directories with ≥ N PRs in the window
   (filters dead code).
  Pros: config-free, repo-agnostic, leader-readable. Cons: sometimes the right
  unit is deeper (e.g. plugin-server/src/main/ingestion/).

  B — Configurable module list (same pattern as criticality). User declares
  "modules" as path globs.
  Pros: precise. Cons: requires user setup.

  C — Compute per file, then surface only the worst offenders ("hot 
  single-author files").
  Pros: no module definition needed. Cons: loses the leader-friendly module
  narrative.

  D — Combine: compute per-directory at depth 2, but allow config override.

  The metric definition itself:

  For each module, compute over the analysis window:
  - Author count (distinct humans, post-bot-filter, post-min-activity)
  - Top-author share (% of PRs in module by single most-active author)
  - Recency-weighted (PRs in last 30 days weighted higher than 6 months ago —
  knowledge fades)

  Then flag modules where: top-author share > 60% AND author count ≤ 2 AND
  module had ≥ N PRs in window.

  My recommendation: D + the metric definition above + clear leader-facing 
  framing.

  1. Default unit: directories at depth 2 (e.g. posthog/api,
  frontend/src/scenes), filtered to those with ≥ 10 PRs in the window. Config
  override for users who want to declare modules manually.
  2. Surface as "Concentration risks" in the dashboard, named by module:
  "plugin-server/ingestion/ — 87% of changes by Carol; 1 other contributor in 6 
  months."
  3. Pair concentration with criticality. A high-criticality module with high
  concentration is a real bus-factor risk (page-the-CTO material). A
  low-criticality module (legacy cron job nobody touches) is fine being
  single-author. Don't surface non-critical concentration as "risk."
  4. At engineer level: surface "areas where you carry the load." On Carol's
  profile: "Sole contributor to ingestion pipeline (high criticality)." This is
  positive framing — recognition of invisible load — and a leader-conversation
  prompt.
  5. Don't score bus factor into the composite. It's a structural team signal,
  not an individual performance signal. Carrying a critical module alone is both
   a strength and a risk; the composite can't capture that ambiguity. Surface it
   on profiles and in the team-health view, but don't add or subtract from the
  score.

  The deeper principle: bus factor is the metric where the dashboard most
  clearly tells leaders something they couldn't see otherwise. It deserves
  prominent placement — probably on the team-overview page as a top-3 panel —
  not buried in individual scoring.

  Do you accept:
  - (a) Module unit = depth-2 directories with activity threshold,
  config-overridable
  - (b) Definition = top-author share + author count + recency weighting,
  flagged when concentration is high AND module is critical
  - (c) Surfaced as "Concentration risks" at team level + "areas you carry" at
  engineer level
  - (d) NOT scored into composite — surfaced as structural signal only

✻ Baked for 47s

❯  I accept (a)–(d). It’s the right way to ship “bus factor” without turning it 
   into a fake performance score.                                               

● Locked. Bus factor as structural signal, not score input.

  We're closing in. Three more branches I want to resolve, starting with the
  most load-bearing.

  ---
  Question 14: "Consistency" is the third leg of your Delivery pillar — and it's
   the most easily faked metric in the whole dashboard.

  You wrote: "Delivery (35%): consistency + cycle time + risk-adjusted 
  throughput."

  Cycle time — locked (Q11). Risk-adjusted throughput — implicitly resolved when
   we redefined risk as blast radius × criticality. But consistency is sitting
  there undefined, and the obvious definitions are all bad.

  The naive definitions and how they fail:

  - "Std deviation of weekly PR count" → an engineer who ships exactly 2 trivial
   PRs every week looks "perfectly consistent." An engineer who ships nothing
  for 3 weeks while doing a deep refactor, then lands one massive impactful PR,
  looks "inconsistent." Rewards rhythm-of-shallow-work over substance.
  - "Active weeks / total weeks in window" → punishes vacation, leave, rotation
  onto non-coding work (incident response, design, hiring). All legitimate.
  - "Variance of cycle time per PR" → penalizes engineers whose work mix varies
  (sometimes a 1-day fix, sometimes a 3-week feature). Variance is the correct
  outcome of doing different kinds of work.
  - "Streak of weeks with ≥ 1 merged PR" → gameable trivially with one
  rubber-stamp PR per week.

  The actual question: what does a leader need to know about consistency that
  they can't get from cycle time + throughput alone?

  I'd argue: almost nothing. "Consistency" as commonly defined is either
  redundant with throughput (rate per active week) or it's a vibe metric that
  punishes legitimate work patterns.

  The options:

  A — Define consistency as "active weeks ratio," acknowledge it punishes
  leave/rotation, surface it as descriptive not scored.

  B — Define consistency as the inverse of cycle-time variance, accept that it
  punishes work-mix variety.

  C — Drop consistency entirely. Delivery = cycle time + risk-adjusted
  throughput. Two legs, not three. Honest about what's measurable.

  D — Replace consistency with "follow-through": ratio of opened PRs that reach
  merge (vs abandoned/closed unmerged). This is a real signal — engineers who
  open lots of PRs and abandon half are different from engineers who finish what
   they start. Different metric than what you originally meant by consistency,
  but more useful.

  My recommendation: C + D.

  1. Drop "consistency" as a Delivery sub-metric. It's not measurable in a way
  that survives Goodhart's law. Two-leg Delivery pillar (cycle time +
  risk-adjusted throughput) is more honest.
  2. Add "follow-through" as a Quality sub-metric instead — ratio of opened PRs
  that reached merge in the window. Surfaces engineers who consistently complete
   vs engineers who churn through abandoned drafts. Genuinely useful, hard to
  game (you can't fake a merge).
  3. Surface "active weeks" as a context statistic on engineer profiles, not
  scored. "Active 9/12 weeks" tells the leader something about availability
  without rewarding/punishing it.
  4. Reweight Delivery → Collaboration → Quality if dropping consistency.
  Originally 35/35/30. With consistency gone, Delivery becomes 2 metrics (cycle
  time + throughput). Either:
    - Keep weights and rebalance sub-metric weights inside Delivery, or
    - Rebalance pillar weights (e.g. 30/35/35) since Quality just got a new
  sub-metric (follow-through).
  5. Make all weights config-overridable with defaults — same pattern as
  everything else. A user who wants 40/30/30 should get it without code changes.

  The principle: if you can't define a metric in a way that survives the
  engineers seeing it, drop it. Don't ship a vibe.

  Do you accept:
  - (a) Drop consistency from Delivery
  - (b) Add follow-through (open→merge ratio) to Quality
  - (c) Active-weeks as context stat, not scored
  - (d) All pillar + sub-metric weights config-overridable with defaults

✻ Worked for 34s

❯  I accept (a)–(d). Your critique of “consistency” is right: most naive        
  definitions are either gameable, punish legitimate life/work mix, or          
  duplicate what throughput + cycle time already say.

  ---
  Question 15 (final): Three settings-level knobs that are too small to grill 
  individually but each has one trap. Resolve all three.

  (i) Self-merge rate edge cases. You listed it as a Collaboration metric ("low
  is better"). The trap: blanket-penalizing self-merge punishes legitimate
  cases.

  The edges:
  - Hotfix / incident response at 2am with no reviewer available — self-merge is
   correct.
  - Single-maintainer modules (legacy code, niche infra) where there's literally
   no one else qualified.
  - Repos requiring 1 review where someone approves and the author merges (you
  may already classify this as "self-merge" if you're checking author == merger,
   but it's actually reviewed).

  My recommendation:
  - Define self-merge as "merged with zero approving reviews from another human"
   — not author==merger. This automatically excludes the third case.
  - Surface as a flag, not a score, when rate exceeds some threshold (default
  20%). Same pattern as the CI-bypass flag in Q12.
  - Don't fold into the Collaboration composite. It's a team-policy/structural
  signal, not an individual quality signal.

  (ii) Analysis window default. Every metric depends on this. Common choices:
  - Rolling 30 days: noisy, vacation-sensitive.
  - Rolling 90 days (quarter): standard, what most leaders think in.
  - Rolling 180 days: smooth but slow to reflect changes.
  - Calendar quarter (Q1, Q2…): aligns with leadership reporting cycles.

  My recommendation: rolling 90 days as default, with the dashboard supporting
  monthly / quarterly / yearly windows side-by-side (you already wanted
  top-contributor views at all three). Calendar-aligned quarters are nice for
  reports but rolling windows are more honest for "how is the team doing right
  now" — a quarter that just started has 5 days of data and tells you nothing.
  Make rolling vs calendar a config toggle.

  (iii) AI summary refresh trigger. You said "weekly + gated refresh." The gate
  is undefined. Options:
  - Manual button ("Regenerate summary") — simplest, leader-controlled,
  predictable cost.
  - Auto-refresh on significant change (e.g., engineer authored a revert, or had
   a high-criticality PR merged) — smart but unpredictable cost and complexity.
  - Weekly cron + manual override — cron for staleness floor, manual button for
  when something material happened.

  My recommendation: weekly cron + manual regenerate button. No "smart"
  auto-refresh in v1 — it adds complexity, makes cost unpredictable, and the
  manual button covers the case where a leader wants a fresh take after a big
  event.

  Do you accept all three:
  - (i) Self-merge = "merged with no human approval," surfaced as flag, not
  scored
  - (ii) Default rolling 90-day window, with monthly/quarterly/yearly views
  side-by-side, rolling-vs-calendar as config toggle
  - (iii) AI summary = weekly cron + manual regenerate button, no auto-refresh
  on events in v1

● All accepted with sound guardrails. The design tree is resolved. Here's the
  consolidated spec from this grilling session — one artifact to take into
  implementation.

  ---
  Resolved design

  Output shape
  - Per-engineer profile cards with pillar radar + composite score
  (GitHub-contributor-card style)
  - "Standouts" panel with 3–5 named axes (not single Top Contributor)
  - Team health overview surfaces Concentration Risks (bus factor) prominently
  - Composite is secondary; pillar breakdown is the primary visual

  Pillars (weights config-overridable, defaults below)
  - Delivery (~30–35%): cycle time (PR open→merge, staged: review-wait /
  iteration / merge-wait, median, draft excluded, stuck-PR flag) + risk-adjusted
   throughput. Consistency dropped.
  - Collaboration (~35%): review load (PRs/active-week), turnaround, breadth,
  depth = "reviews that triggered code change" + comment volume secondary.
  Changes-requested rate shown unscored. Self-merge as flag, not score. "Rigor" 
  dropped.
  - Quality (~30–35%): revert-within-2-weeks (primary), follow-through
  (open→merge ratio), team-level blame attribution only. Per-engineer blame 
  rejected. Bug-fix ratio shown unscored. CI cleanliness team-level only with 
  required-check detection caveat.

  Risk model
  - Risk = blast radius, not LOC: criticality × files × cross-module breadth ×
  test-coverage ratio
  - Criticality = config-driven path globs with sensible defaults;
  user-overridable

  Bus factor / Knowledge gaps
  - Unit: depth-2 directories with activity threshold; config-overridable
  - Metric: top-author share + author count + recency-weighted; flagged when
  concentration high AND criticality high
  - Surfaced as "Concentration Risks" (team) and "Areas you carry" (engineer)
  - Not scored into composite

  Work mix lens
  - Three buckets: Capability / Fix / Maintenance
  - Waterfall classifier: Conventional Commits → label → keyword → optional LLM
  fallback (cached forever per PR)
  - Descriptive only, never scored; framed as "Work mix" not "decision-making
  patterns"
  - Confidence indicator when classification coverage is low

  Cohort definition
  - Bot exclusion via config + defaults (*[bot], dependabot, renovate, etc.)
  - Min activity threshold (default 3 PRs / 4 weeks; M=3 for monthly view)
  - All rate metrics normalized on active weeks
  - Identity dedup punted to v2
  - Configurable review-eligibility for bot vs external PRs

  AI summary
  - Inputs: metrics + cited evidence (top PR titles, file paths, reverts,
  bus-factor flags, work mix)
  - Structured output: Strengths / Concerns / Watch-items, claims must cite PR#
  or file path
  - Generator never sees composite score (no reverse-justification)
  - Weekly cron staleness floor + manual regenerate button
  - Rate-limit manual regen (1–3/day); cache key = inputs hash + model version
  - Evidence panel collapsible under summary for sanity-check

  Time / windows
  - Default rolling 90 days
  - Monthly / 90-day / yearly views side-by-side for standouts and trends
  - Rolling vs calendar as config toggle; coverage indicator ("12 days in
  window") shown in calendar mode

  Operational flags (not scored)
  - Self-merge rate >20% — conversation prompt
  - Merged with bypassed required checks — only when required-check context
  reliably detectable
  - Bot approvals / automerge labeled separately when detectable
  - Inactive in recent period — surfaced as context, no auto-exclusion

  Explicitly dropped
  - AI-assisted code detection
  - Innovation rate
  - Roadmap alignment (v1)
  - Capitalizable
  - Talent density
  - Issue-to-merge cycle time (DORA lead time)
  - Per-engineer blame attribution
  - Per-engineer CI scoring
  - Single "Top Contributor" leaderboard

  Ready for implementation planning when you are. Per your global preferences,
  I'll present an investigation/plan before any code edits — say the word and
  I'll start scoping v1 against the existing server/impact*.ts files.

❯ Yeah so composite still on card but you say it “secondary”, that ok only if   
  big visual is three pillar not one number, better rename or hide default so   
  people not instantly do leaderboard brain; radar look cool but busy boss      
  maybe just want three bar + number for quick read. Test coverage in risk      
  model is nice but many repo missing or messy so don’t hard depend, degrade    
  soft. “Review cause code change” need very clear rule for squash/force push   
  else metric lie sometimes, write the caveats loud. Follow-through need exact  
  rule which PR opened when and counted merge when, and kick draft junk out.    
  Rolling vs calendar fine but don’t mix meaning for standouts,  same window    
  logic everywhere or you compare apple orange. what do you think?              

● All five are correct and the design is sharper for them. Locking with crisp
  rules:

  (1) Visual hierarchy — "leaderboard brain" defense.
  Yes. Demote composite hard:
  - Primary visual on profile card: three horizontal bars (Delivery /
  Collaboration / Quality) with the metric values, not a radar. Bars beat radar
  for cognitive speed — busy leader scans left-to-right in 2 seconds.
  - Composite number: hidden behind a toggle by default, or shown as small text
  below pillars labeled "blended score (configurable weights)" — never as the
  headline.
  - Standouts page leads with the reason, not the score.
  Drop the radar from v1. It's pretty, not useful.

  (2) Risk model: soft-degrade test coverage.
  Yes. Risk components are independent and rebalance when one is unavailable:
  - If test files can't be reliably detected (no obvious test/, __tests__/,
  *_test.*, *.spec.* patterns in the repo), drop the test-ratio factor entirely
  and rebalance remaining factors (criticality × files × cross-module breadth)
  to sum to 1.
  - Surface in the UI: "Risk model: test-coverage signal unavailable for this
  repo" — honest about the gap.
  - Same soft-degrade pattern for any optional input (CI data, labels,
  conventional-commits prefixes).

  (3) "Reviews that triggered code change" — squash/force-push rules.
  Yes, this is where the metric lies if you're not careful. Explicit rules:
  - Source: PR timeline events, not git log on the merge commit. Squash merges
  flatten history; force pushes rewrite it. Both destroy the signal if read
  post-merge.
  - A review is counted as "triggered change" if new commits (or force-pushed 
  head SHA changes) appear on the PR's head ref between the review submitted_at 
  and the merge_at. PR timeline gives both events with timestamps and SHAs —
  that's the source of truth.
  - Force pushes count as change even if the diff is identical (we can't cheaply
   prove diff equivalence; treat any head SHA change as a code-iteration
  signal).
  - Caveats panel in the UI documents this explicitly: "Squash-merge and 
  force-push handling: detection uses PR timeline events; force-pushes count as 
  iteration even if diff is unchanged."
  - Reviews submitted after the last code change (i.e. final approval reviews)
  are excluded from the depth denominator — they can't "trigger change" by
  definition. Counting them would punish reviewers who approve clean PRs.

  (4) Follow-through: exact rules.
  Yes. Tight definition to avoid draft-churn distorting the metric:
  - Numerator: PRs merged within the analysis window.
  - Denominator: PRs that left draft state within the window (i.e. became "ready
   for review"). A PR opened-and-closed-still-as-draft is excluded — that's WIP
  exploration, not abandoned work.
  - PR open-time for purposes of cycle time / follow-through = ready_for_review
  event timestamp (or created_at if PR was never a draft).
  - Counted as merged = merged_at is non-null and within window.
  - Counted as abandoned = closed without merge, after leaving draft. These hit
  the denominator but not the numerator — that's the signal.
  - Re-opened PRs: count once, by final state.

  (5) Window consistency — no apples-vs-oranges.
  Yes, critical and easy to get wrong. Rule:
  - Window mode (rolling vs calendar) is a global dashboard setting, not
  per-panel. User picks once; every view, every standout, every trend uses the
  same logic.
  - The size of the window varies per view (monthly view = 30d, quarterly = 90d,
   yearly = 365d) — but the mode (rolling-from-now vs calendar-aligned) is
  locked globally.
  - Standouts at different time windows are comparable to each other only within
   a mode. Toggling rolling↔calendar invalidates standouts cache and forces
  recompute (don't show stale data from the other mode).
  - UI surfaces the mode prominently in the header: "Rolling 90d" or "Q2 2026
  (calendar, 41 days in)" — so the leader always knows what they're looking at.

  lib/core/Api.ts — proposed shape

  Per your spec (functions, not const arrows; object export):

  async function get<T>(url: string): Promise<T> {
    const res = await fetch(url)
    if (!res.ok) throw new Error(`GET ${url} failed: ${res.status}`)
    return res.json() as Promise<T>
  }

  async function post<T>(url: string, body: unknown): Promise<T> {
    const res = await fetch(url, {
      method: 'POST',
      headers: { 'Content-Type': 'application/json' },
      body: JSON.stringify(body)
    })
    if (!res.ok) throw new Error(`POST ${url} failed: ${res.status}`)
    return res.json() as Promise<T>
  }

  export const Api = { get, post }

  Then client/todo/Todo.ts and future client modules collapse to
  Api.get('/api/todos') etc.

  Open questions before I write arch.md and Api.ts:

  1. Scope of Api: just get/post for now, or include patch/del too since Todo.ts
   already uses them? My recommendation: include all four — keeps the wrapper
  complete and migrating Todo.ts becomes one-line-per-call.
  2. Error handling: throw on non-2xx (shown above) vs return { data, error }?
  Recommendation: throw. Callers wrap in try/catch where needed; cleaner types.
  3. arch.md location: repo root, as a flat doc? Recommendation: yes, root
  arch.md.
  4. arch.md scope: full design tree from grilling + module map above, or

  migrate client/todo/Todo.ts to use it as the proof-of-pattern.

❯ let's first start with BE                                                     

● Good — BE first. Before writing code, three sharp questions to nail down the
  first BE slice.

  Proposed BE build order

  1. DB schema (authors, prs, reviews, files, prFiles, syncState) — foundation
  2. Config (bots, criticality globs, weights, target repo) — small, needed by
  everything
  3. GitHub ingest (Github.ts + Sync.ts) — REST API, incremental with cursor
  4. Cohort filter (bot exclusion, activity threshold)
  5. Sub-metrics (Cycle, Review, Risk, Quality, WorkMix, BusFactor) — one at a
  time
  6. Pillars + Standouts (combine into the dashboard payload)
  7. Dashboard route (Hono endpoints exposing the computed payload)

  I want to scope the first slice tight: DB schema + GitHub PR ingest + a 
  /api/sync route + a /api/prs debug route to verify data lands. Reviews, files,
   and metrics come in subsequent slices.

  Open questions before I plan the first slice

  Q1 — GitHub auth. PostHog is public so technically unauth works, but the
  unauth rate limit is 60/hr — useless for any real backfill. Recommendation:
  personal access token via GITHUB_TOKEN env var, fine-grained, read-only on the
   target repo. Loaded in Config.ts. Agree?

  Q2 — Target repo: hardcoded or config? Two options:
  - (a) Hardcoded posthog/posthog in v1; config in v2. Faster to ship.
  - (b) Config from day 1 (config.json or env var like
  TARGET_REPO=posthog/posthog). One extra small file, but means the tool is
  repo-agnostic from the start.

  Recommendation: (b) — you said "generic" earlier; baking that in from day 1
  costs nothing now and saves a refactor later.

  Q3 — First-slice fetch strategy: REST or GraphQL?
  - REST: simpler, well-documented, 1 PR = 1 request for details. ~5-10 PRs/sec
  on a token. PostHog has ~25k PRs — backfill takes ~1hr.
  - GraphQL: one big paginated query can pull PR + reviews + files in a single
  response. Faster but more complex (cursor handling, query construction, error
  modes).

  Recommendation: REST for v1. Start with GET 
  /repos/{owner}/{repo}/pulls?state=closed&sort=updated&per_page=100. We can
  layer GraphQL later for review/file enrichment if rate-limit pain warrants it.
   The simpler path lets us see real data quickly.

  Confirm Q1 / Q2 / Q3 and I'll write the first-slice plan with concrete schema
  columns, the Github fetcher shape, and the sync-cursor strategy — then
  implement.

  ⎿  Plan saved to: ~/.claude/plans/purring-munching-toucan.md · /plan to edit
     Impact Dashboard — Bootstrap + First Slice

     Context

     We are building an engineering impact dashboard for a GitHub repo (PostHog
     as the primary target, but the tool must be repo-agnostic). The audience is
      busy engineering leaders who want meaningful signal beyond LOC / commit /
     PR counts.

     A detailed 15-question grilling session resolved the design (see arch.md at
      repo root). The agreed shape:

     - Three pillars (Delivery / Collaboration / Quality), each composed of
     structurally honest sub-metrics that survive Goodhart's law.
     - Risk = blast radius (criticality × files × cross-module breadth × test
     ratio), not LOC.
     - Bus factor + work mix as descriptive panels, not scored.
     - AI summary grounded in cited evidence, weekly cached.
     - Standouts (multi-axis) instead of a single Top Contributor leaderboard.
     - Soft-degrade on every optional input (test files, CI data, labels).

     The current repo is a Svelte 5 + Hono + Drizzle + SQLite starter with a
     Todo example. The Todo example is scaffolding to be removed before
     dashboard work begins.

     Bootstrap (already executed before plan mode kicked in)

     - arch.md written at repo root with all decisions from the grilling
     session.
     - lib/core/Api.ts created with get / post wrappers (named functions, object
      export, throw on non-2xx) — matches the user's stated convention (export 
     const Api = { get, post }).
     - Removed: client/todo/ (folder), server/Todos.ts, todos table in
     db/schema.ts, todo type in client/types.ts.
     - Updated: server/index.ts (dropped Todos init), client/app/App.svelte
     (dropped Todo route), client/app/Header.svelte (dropped Todos link).
     - db/schema.ts and client/types.ts left as empty placeholders so dashboard
     schema/types can land cleanly.
     - grep confirmed no lingering Todo references in .ts / .svelte files.

     First Slice — Backend foundation: schema + GitHub PR ingest

     User chose to start with backend before any frontend work. Locked answers:
     - Auth: GITHUB_TOKEN env var, fine-grained PAT, read-only on target repo.
     - Target repo: config-driven from day 1 (no hardcode).
     - Fetch: REST API for v1 (GraphQL deferred).

     This slice gets real PR data landing in SQLite with an incremental cursor.
     Reviews / files / metrics come in later slices.

     Files to create

     server/
       Config.ts        — reads GITHUB_TOKEN from env, target repo from config
     file,
                          bot exclusion list. Pattern: function load(): Config;
     export const Config = { load }.

       Github.ts        — REST client for the GitHub PRs endpoint.
                          Pulls
     /repos/{owner}/{repo}/pulls?state=all&sort=updated&direction=asc
                          paginated, with token auth. Functions:
     fetchPullsSince(cursor),
                          returns batches of raw PR objects + next cursor.
                          Handles 401/403/404 with clear errors.
                          Handles 403/429 rate-limit (Retry-After /
     X-RateLimit-Reset) by waiting.

       Sync.ts          — orchestrates incremental ingest. Reads cursor from
     syncState,
                          loops fetchPullsSince batches, normalizes + upserts
     into db,
                          writes new cursor. Exposes function syncPulls():
     Promise<{ added: number }>.

       Pulls.ts         — Hono routes:
                            POST /api/sync   — triggers Sync.syncPulls, returns
     count
                            GET  /api/pulls  — debug: returns last N rows
     (limit, offset)
                          Pattern: function init(app, db) + export const Pulls =
      { init }.

     Files to update

     db/schema.ts       — define tables:
                            authors    (login PK, name, isBot flag derived at
     insert)
                            pulls      (id PK = GitHub PR node_id, number, repo,
      title,
                                        state, author_login FK→authors, body,
     createdAt,
                                        updatedAt, closedAt, mergedAt,
     draftCreatedAt nullable,
                                        isDraft, mergedBy, baseRef, headRef,
     labels JSON,
                                        additions, deletions, changedFiles)
                            syncState  (key PK, value text, updatedAt) — stores
     cursor like
                                        { key: 'pulls:posthog/posthog', value:
     '<iso-timestamp>' }

     config.json        — at repo root, gitignored sample committed as
     config.example.json:
                            {
                              "repo": "posthog/posthog",
                              "bots": ["dependabot*", "renovate*",
     "github-actions*", "*[bot]"]
                            }
                          Config.load reads this; later slices add criticality /
      weights.

     .env.example       — GITHUB_TOKEN=ghp_xxx

     server/index.ts    — import Db, Pulls; call Pulls.init(app, Db.get()).

     .gitignore         — add config.json, .env (verify existing entries first).

     Drizzle / DB setup

     - db/schema.ts uses existing sqliteTable pattern (matches removed todos
     table style).
     - Run npm run db:push to materialize the schema in data/sqlite.db.
     - server/Db.ts already wired correctly — no changes needed.

     Github fetch contract

     - Endpoint: GET https://api.github.com/repos/{owner}/{repo}/pulls?state=all
     &sort=updated&direction=asc&per_page=100&page=N
     - Headers: Authorization: Bearer ${GITHUB_TOKEN}, Accept: 
     application/vnd.github+json, X-GitHub-Api-Version: 2022-11-28.
     - Pagination: walk page= until response is empty.
     - Cursor: stored as the max updated_at seen. Next sync resumes by filtering
      client-side (REST endpoint sorts ascending by updated; we stop persisting
     once we've processed everything > cursor).
     - Note: the list endpoint does NOT return additions, deletions,
     changed_files, or merge metadata. Those need a per-PR detail fetch (GET 
     /repos/{owner}/{repo}/pulls/{number}). For this slice we store what the
     list endpoint gives us and leave detail-fetch fields nullable — detail
     enrichment is a separate slice (it costs 1 request per PR, so it's
     expensive and needs its own backfill strategy).

     Conventions to follow (from existing repo)

     - Feature modules: named-function declarations, single object export (e.g.
     export const Sync = { syncPulls }).
     - No arrow-const for functions. Explicit types on args / returns. No any.
     - Use path aliases $server, $db, $lib.
     - Soft-degrade: if GITHUB_TOKEN missing, Config.load throws with a clear
     actionable error — do not silently fall back to unauth (60/hr is useless).

     Out of scope for this slice

     - Reviews, files, check runs (next slices).
     - PR detail enrichment (additions/deletions/merge metadata) — separate
     slice with its own rate-limit plan.
     - Cohort filtering, metrics, pillars, AI summary, dashboard route, any
     frontend work.
     - GraphQL ingest.

     Verification

     - GITHUB_TOKEN=... npm run dev — server boots, no crash.
     - curl -X POST http://localhost:8080/api/sync — returns { added: N } where
     N > 0. Watch logs for rate-limit handling if backfill is large.
     - curl 'http://localhost:8080/api/pulls?limit=5' — returns 5 most recent
     PRs with the expected fields populated.
     - sqlite3 data/sqlite.db 'select count(*) from pulls' — non-zero.
     - Second /api/sync call — added: 0 (cursor works; no re-ingest).
     - npm run check — TS clean.
     - npm run lint — prettier + eslint clean.

     Critical files referenced

     - arch.md — full design tree.
     - lib/core/Api.ts — client wrapper (not used this slice; lands when
     frontend starts).
     - lib/serve.ts — existing Hono dev/prod server, no changes needed.
     - server/Db.ts — existing Drizzle setup, no changes needed.
     - server/index.ts — register Pulls routes here.
     - db/schema.ts — currently empty placeholder; this slice fills it.

● Slice complete. Lint + typecheck both clean. Schema is materialized in
  data/sqlite.db.

  Files created
  - server/Config.ts — env + JSON config loader, throws if GITHUB_TOKEN missing
  - server/Github.ts — REST client with recursive rate-limit retry
  - server/Sync.ts — recursive paginated ingest with cursor in sync_state
  - server/Pulls.ts — POST /api/sync, GET /api/pulls
  - config.example.json — repo + bot globs (defaults to posthog/posthog)
  - .env.empty — GITHUB_TOKEN=

  Files modified
  - db/schema.ts — authors, pulls, sync_state tables
  - server/index.ts — wires Pulls.init
  - .gitignore — adds config.json
  - client/app/Header.svelte — typed links + keyed each (was failing
  pre-existing lint)

  Conventions enforced by lint (good to know): no let, no for/for...of, no
  mutating array methods, no classes. Sync.syncPage and Github.request use
  recursion accordingly.

  To verify end-to-end (you'll need to do these):
  cp .env.empty .env       # then put your GITHUB_TOKEN in
  cp config.example.json config.json   # optional, only if you want to change
  repo/bots
  export $(cat .env | xargs) && npm run dev
  curl -X POST http://localhost:8080/api/sync       # returns { added: N }
  curl 'http://localhost:8080/api/pulls?limit=5'    # 5 most recent
  curl -X POST http://localhost:8080/api/sync       # second time → { added: 0 }

  The dev script doesn't auto-load .env. If you want it to, I can add
